<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Large-scale gene losses underlie the genome evolution of parasitic plant Cuscuta australis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-07-11"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Dodders (Cuscuta spp., Convolvulaceae) are root- and leafless parasitic plants. The physiology, ecology, and evolution of these obligate parasites are poorly understood. A high-quality reference genome of Cuscuta australis was assembled. Our analyses reveal that Cuscuta experienced accelerated molecular evolution, and Cuscuta and the convolvulaceous morning glory (Ipomoea) shared a common whole-genome triplication event before their divergence. C. australis genome harbors 19,671 protein-coding genes, and importantly, 11.7% of the conserved orthologs in autotrophic plants are lost in C. australis. Many of these gene loss events likely result from its parasitic lifestyle and the massive changes of its body plan. Moreover, comparison of the gene expression patterns in Cuscuta prehaustoria/haustoria and various tissues of closely related autotrophic plants suggests that Cuscuta haustorium formation requires mostly genes normally involved in root development. The C. australis genome provides important resources for studying the evolution of parasitism, regressive evolution, and evo-devo in plant parasites. Dodders (Cuscuta spp., Convolvulaceae) are root- and leafless parasitic plants. Here, the authors sequence the genome of Cuscuta australis and find remarkable gene loss associated with parasitic lifestyle and large changes in body plan."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Dodders (<i>Cuscuta</i> spp., Convolvulaceae) are root- and leafless parasitic plants. The physiology, ecology, and evolution of these obligate parasites are poorly understood. A high-quality reference genome of <i>Cuscuta australis</i> was assembled. Our analyses reveal that <i>Cuscuta</i> experienced accelerated molecular evolution, and <i>Cuscuta</i> and the convolvulaceous morning glory (<i>Ipomoea</i>) shared a common whole-genome triplication event before their divergence. <i>C. australis</i> genome harbors 19,671 protein-coding genes, and importantly, 11.7% of the conserved orthologs in autotrophic plants are lost in <i>C. australis</i>. Many of these gene loss events likely result from its parasitic lifestyle and the massive changes of its body plan. Moreover, comparison of the gene expression patterns in <i>Cuscuta</i> prehaustoria/haustoria and various tissues of closely related autotrophic plants suggests that <i>Cuscuta</i> haustorium formation requires mostly genes normally involved in root development. The <i>C. australis</i> genome provides important resources for studying the evolution of parasitism, regressive evolution, and evo-devo in plant parasites.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>About 1% of the flowering plants are haustorial parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Westwood, J. H., Yoder, J. I., Timko, M. P. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. The evolution of parasitism in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. 15, 227–235 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d46651e576">1</a></sup>, and some are responsible for severe yield losses in many crops. Although the evolutionary history of plant parasitism remains elusive, all parasitic plants, except the mycoheterotrophs, use a specialized haustorial organ to extract water and nutrients through vascular connections with the hosts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yoshida, S., Cui, S., Ichihashi, Y. &amp; Shirasu, K. The haustorium, a specialized invasive organ in parasitic plants. Annu. Rev. Plant. Biol. 67, 643–667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d46651e580">2</a></sup>. The <i>Cuscuta</i> spp. (dodders) are typical obligate shoot parasites widely distributed worldwide comprising ~194 species, and is the only genus of parasites in Convolvulaceae (Solanales). <i>Cuscuta</i> spp. exhibit massive changes of their body plans, being leaf- and rootless throughout their lifecycles (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig1">1</a>a). They contain trace amounts of chlorophyll, but cannot sustain themselves from their own photosynthesis. Although whether <i>Cuscuta</i> plants are hemi- or holoparasites remains debatable, they could be considered to be transitioning from hemiparasitism to holoparasitism. Through haustoria, dodders not only obtain water and nutrients, but secondary metabolites, mRNAs, and proteins from their host plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E. K., Depamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808–811 (2014)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d46651e597">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Birschwilks, M., Haupt, S., Hofius, D. &amp; Neumann, S. Transfer of phloem-mobile substances from the host plants to the holoparasite Cuscuta sp. J. Exp. Bot. 57, 911–921 (2006)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d46651e597_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Smith, J. D., Woldemariam, M. G., Mescher, M. C., Jander, G. &amp; De Moraes, C. M. Glucosinolates from host plants influence growth of the parasitic plant Cuscuta gronovii and its susceptibility to aphid feeding. Plant Physiol. 172, 181–197 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46651e600">5</a></sup>. These features make <i>Cuscuta</i> an important model to elucidate plant–parasite interactions and the evolution of plant parasites.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="619" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Morphological traits and genome structure of <i>C. australis</i>. <b>a</b> Photographs of <i>C. australis</i> seed (1), seedling (2), vines twining around the wild tomato <i>Solanum pennellii</i> (3 &amp; 4; partial haustoria can be seen in 3), flowers (5), and seed capsules (6). <b>b</b> Phylogenetic tree generated from genome-wide one-to-one orthogroups (bootstrap values for all clades are 100%). <b>c</b> Circos plot of a set of syntenic genome segments of <i>C. australis</i>, Japanese morning glory, and coffee. Numbers besides terminals of each karyotype denote the start and end of chromosome segment or contigs with unit of Mb. <b>d</b> Numbers of gene clades (shown on top of the trees) supporting different hypotheses on the order of speciation and whole-genome triplication event in the <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Ipomoea</i> lineage</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here we sequence the genome of <i>Cuscuta australis</i>. Our analyses reveal that the genome of <i>C. australis</i> experienced massive gene losses, including important genes involved in leaf and root development, flowering-time control, as well as defense against pathogens and insects. Comparison of <i>Cuscuta</i> haustorium/prehaustorium gene expression patterns with the tissues from closely related autotrophic plants suggests that <i>Cuscuta</i> haustorium formation likely requires genes that are normally involved in root development.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>The genome size of <i>C. australis</i> was estimated to be 272.57 Mb from <i>k</i>-mer analysis. We next generated 26.6 Gb (97.6-fold coverage) of <i>C. australis</i> genome sequences from a single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing platform. The <i>C. australis</i> genome sequence includes 249 contigs (N50 = 3.63 Mb), and these contigs were further assembled to form 103 scaffolds (N50 = 5.95 Mb; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">1</a>). In total, 264.83 Mb (219 contigs) of nuclear sequences (97.16% of the estimated genome size) and 1.9 Mb (30 contigs) of organellar sequences were acquired (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">2</a>). The accuracy and heterozygosity were estimated to be 99.99% and 0.013%, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">3</a>). A total of 155 Mb of repetitive elements were identified in the <i>C. australis</i> nuclear genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">4</a>). Comparison with <i>Ipomoea nil</i> (Japanese morning glory; also Convolvulaceae), which is relatively closely related, indicated similar proportions of different types of repetitive elements between the two genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">4</a>), although the <i>I. nil</i> genome contains many more repeats (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">4</a>), consistent with its larger genome size (734 Mb). LTR (long terminal repeats) retrotransposons are the most dominant type of repeats in both <i>C. australis</i> and <i>I. nil</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">4</a>), and we specifically inspected the <i>LTR/Gypsy</i> and <i>LTR/Copia</i> superfamilies. These two genomes exhibit large differences in <i>LTR/Gypsy</i> (84,083 in <i>I. nil</i> and 24,453 in <i>C. australis</i>) and <i>LTR/Copia</i> (96,355 in <i>I. nil</i> and 43,853 in <i>C. australis</i>) copy numbers; moreover, the sequences of <i>LTR/Gypsy</i> and <i>LTR/Copia</i> members have also diverged as revealed by phylogenetic analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>Based on de novo gene structure prediction, homology comparison, and transcript data of both <i>C. australis</i> and the closely related relative <i>C. pentagona</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186–1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46651e776">6</a></sup>, 19671 genes could be annotated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Phylogeny analysis</h3><p>The phylogenetic position of <i>Cuscuta</i> was determined using 1796 one-to-one orthogroups (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1b</a>) identified from <i>C. australis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and six lamiids plants—the Solanales <i>Ipomoea nil</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> (potato), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato), and <i>Capsicum annuum</i> (pepper), the Gentianales <i>Coffea canephora</i> (coffee), and the Lamiales <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (monkey flower) (for simplicity, these seven autotrophic species are collectively named 7Ref-Species). Consistent with their phylogenetic relationship, <i>Cuscuta</i> forms a sister group with <i>Ipomoea</i>, and we estimated that these two lineages split ~33 million years ago (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">2</a>). Notably, <i>Cuscuta</i> shows a much longer branch than does <i>Ipomoea</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig1">1b</a>), providing genome-wide evidence consistent with the hypothesis that parasitic plants evolve rapidly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Estep, M. C., Gowda, B. S., Huang, K., Timko, M. P. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genomic characterization for parasitic weeds of the genus Striga by sample sequence analysis. Plant Genome 5, 30–41 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46651e838">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bromham, L., Cowman, P. F. &amp; Lanfear, R. Parasitic plants have increased rates of molecular evolution across all three genomes. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 126 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d46651e841">8</a></sup>. This result is statistically significant by both two cluster analysis and relative rate test (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">6</a>). Previously, a whole-genome duplication (WGD) event was detected in <i>Ipomoea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hoshino, A. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat. Commun. 7, 13295 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46651e854">9</a></sup>. The syntenic blocks and trees of the syntenic gene groups of <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Ipomoea</i> vs. <i>Coffea</i> genome indicate that <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Ipomoea</i> experienced a whole-genome triplication event before their divergence from a common ancestor (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig1">1c, d</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">3</a> to 5, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Contractions and expansions of gene families</h3><p>Parasitism and large changes of body plan in <i>Cuscuta</i> suggest that many gene families might have experienced substantial alterations in sizes, including those that function in leaf and root physiology. To study gene family expansion and contraction, a rigorous bioinformatic pipeline was adopted to identify gene families (details see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">5</a>). In addition, the genome of <i>Utricularia gibba</i> (Lentibulariaceae; an aquatic carnivorous bladderwort plant) was included in the analysis, given that <i>U. gibba</i> also exhibits large changes in body plan (e.g., no true roots). We identified a total of 13981 gene families in <i>C. australis</i>, <i>U. gibba</i>, and the 7Ref-Species (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1a</a>); among these, 1256 and 478 families in <i>C. australis</i> and 605 and 848 families in <i>U. gibba</i> were found to have had significant contractions and expansions, respectively, revealed by a maximum-likelihood analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1a</a>). Moreover, box plots of the <i>F</i>-indices (details see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">5</a>), which describe the differences among the gene numbers of the conserved gene families in the 7Ref-Species (namely, in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and at least five of the six remaining autotrophic species), indicate that in <i>C. australi</i>s and <i>U. gibba</i>, gene numbers in 72% and 62% of the conserved gene families are below the averages, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="280" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Expansion and contraction in <i>C. australis</i> gene families. <b>a</b> Significantly expanded and contracted gene families. Brackets above each branch indicate numbers of expanded (in black, before comma) and contracted (in red, after comma) gene families. <b>b</b> Tukey boxplot overview of the differences among the gene numbers of the conserved gene families, based on the <i>F</i>-index values (<i>F</i>-indices range from 0 to 1; when <i>F</i> = , &lt; , or &gt; 0.5, the gene number in the given gene family is equal to, smaller, or greater than the average size of this gene family in all species). The left and right sides of the boxes are the first and third quartiles, respectively; means and medians of the data are shown as an “ × ” and the bands in the boxes, respectively; for each box, the whiskers represent the smallest and biggest datum that are still within 1.5 times interquartile range of the lower and upper quartile, and the outliers are shown as dots</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Overall gene losses</h3><p>The drastic contractions of gene families in <i>C. australi</i>s and <i>U. gibba</i> suggest considerable gene losses in their genomes. Next, BUSCO analysis <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d46651e996">10</a></sup> was carried out to map the 1440 conserved orthologs in land plants to the genomes of 7Ref-Species, <i>C. australi</i>s, and <i>U. gibba</i>. Consistent with their contracted gene families, the missing BUSCOs in <i>C. australi</i>s and <i>U. gibba</i> (16.30% and 13.70%, respectively) are more than those in the 7Ref-Species (1.40% to 8.50%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>To identify specific orthologous genes that are lost in <i>C. australis</i> and <i>U. gibba</i>, we developed a stringent genome-wide analysis pipeline to divide each gene family into small orthogroups using a method combining phylogenetic and syntenic analysis (details see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">6</a>) and the functional annotations were assigned using <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome as the reference (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">6</a>). This analysis resulted in 21487 orthogroups (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1b</a>). Among the 11995 conserved orthogroups in the 7Ref-Species, there are 1402 and 1555 orthogroups whose <i>C. australis</i> and <i>U. gibba</i> members are absent, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1b and 1c</a>). Strikingly, 563 orthogroups have no <i>C. australis</i> and <i>U. gibba</i> orthologs, whose functions include phytohormone pathways, nutrient uptake, defense response, and root hair development (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2a</a>); 839 orthogroups specifically lost their members in <i>C. australis</i>, and these genes are mainly involved in response to light, photosynthesis, chloroplast RNA processing, and adventitious root development (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2b</a>); 992 orthogroups specifically have no members of <i>U. gibba</i>, and genes in these orthogroups are involved in signaling, response to stimuli, and protein modifications, among others (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2c</a>). In addition to the lost genes, in <i>C. australis</i> genome we identified 1168 pseudogenes, which contain frame-shifts or premature stop codons. Among these, we found five flowering-time-related genes and 13 photosynthesis-related genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3a</a>).</p><p>We next inspected the tissue-specific expression patterns of the <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>I. nil</i> (the closest autotrophs to <i>Cuscuta</i> among the 7Ref-Species) orthologs in the 1402 orthogroups, whose <i>Cuscuta</i> members are lost (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1d</a>). It was found that these orthologs’ principally expressed tissues (the tissues, in which the expression levels of a given gene are at least 1-fold greater than the averages of its expression levels in other tissues, are defined as the principally expressed tissues for that gene) are the leaves and root of both <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>I. nil</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3a</a>). These data are consistent with the leaf- and rootless body plan of <i>Cuscuta</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04721-8/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4721_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="793" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Gene losses in <i>C. australis</i>. <b>a</b> The principally expressed tissues (PETs) of the respective orthologs of <i>C. australis</i> lost genes in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>I. nil</i>. The boxes represent different tissues. The respective PETs of the orthogroups, which have no <i>C. australis</i> members, were identified in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>I. nil</i>. The numbers of orthogroups, which have PETs in leaves, roots, flowers, and other tissues, are shown in the boxes, and the respective percentages (proportional to the areas of the boxes) indicate the ratios between these indicated numbers and the numbers of all orthogroups whose PETs were identified to be the corresponding tissues of <i>I. nil</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. <b>b</b> Simplified gene network controlling flowering time. Genes in green boxes are retained in <i>C. australis</i>, and the lost ones are in orange boxes. Arrows and T-ends represent promoting and inhibiting genetic interactions, respectively, and round dots at both ends symbolize genetic interactions with unknown directions. <b>c</b> Numbers of genes in the gene families of <i>R</i> genes, <i>TPS</i>s, <i>P450</i>s, and <i>RLK</i>s</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Loss of genes for leaf and root development</h3><p>Next, the <i>C. australis</i> genome was specifically searched for genes that mediate leaf and root development, and it was found that a number of important genes involved in leaf and/or root development are absent (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1c</a>): The orthologs of (1) <i>LCR</i> (leaf shape and vein formation); (2) <i>TRN1/LOP1</i> (leaf patterning and lateral root development); (3) <i>PLT1</i>, <i>2</i>, and <i>5</i> (specification of root stem cell niche); (4) <i>SMB</i> (lateral root cap maturation); (5) all the <i>CASP</i> genes which are required for Casparian strip formation; (6) <i>WAK1</i> to <i>5</i> (root cell expansion); (7) <i>WOX3</i>, <i>5</i>, and <i>7</i> (embryonic patterning, stem cell maintenance, and organ formation).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Loss of genes for nutrient uptake</h3><p>We found that many genes involved in potassium (K), phosphate (P), and nitrate (N) uptake from soil are lost in <i>C. australis</i>. For K<sup>+</sup> uptake: the orthologs of <i>HAK5</i> (a high affinity K<sup>+</sup> transporter), <i>KAT3/AtKC1</i> (a general regulatory negatively modulating many inward Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channels), and <i>CHX20</i> (a Na<sup>+</sup>(K<sup>+</sup>)/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter) are absent in <i>C. australis</i>. The following orthologs important for P uptake are also lost: <i>PHO2</i> (P uptake and translocation), <i>PHT2;1</i> (a chloroplast P transporter), and <i>SPX3</i> (P signaling). Several genes involved in N uptake are missing as well, such as the orthologs of <i>NAXT1</i> (a nitrate efflux transporter), and <i>NLP7</i> (a positive regulator for nitrate-induced gene expression); moreover, while the low-affinity nitrate transporters <i>NRT1.1</i> and <i>1.2</i> are retained, the high-affinity nitrate transporters <i>NRT2.1</i> and <i>2.2</i> and <i>NRT3.1</i> are absent.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Loss of photosynthesis genes</h3><p><i>C. australis</i> has very limited photosynthetic capability (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">7</a>). Among 248 photosynthesis-related genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> annotated by GO, 38 <i>C. australis</i> orthologs are missing (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1c</a>). The plastome of <i>C. australis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">8</a>) appears to have gene contractions (81 genes remain, while the plastome of <i>I. nil</i> harbors 104 genes), and is similar to those of the previously sequenced <i>Cuscuta</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Funk, H. T., Berg, S., Krupinska, K., Maier, U. G. &amp; Krause, K. Complete DNA sequences of the plastid genomes of two parasitic flowering plant species, Cuscuta reflexa and Cuscuta gronovii. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 45 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1334">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="McNeal, J. R., Arumugunathan, K., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; Depamphilis, C. W. Systematics and plastid genome evolution of the cryptically photosynthetic parasitic plant genus Cuscuta (Convolvulaceae). BMC Biol. 5, 55 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1337">12</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM7">4</a>), especially <i>C. obtusiflora</i> and <i>C. gronovii</i>, all of these belong to the subgenus <i>Grammica</i> with <i>C. australis</i>. Notably, all these <i>Cuscuta</i> plastomes lack <i>ndh</i> genes encoding proteins for forming the NADH dehydrogenase complex functioning in electron cycling around photosystem I under stress. These data concur with the generally accepted notion that <i>ndh</i> genes are first to be lost in the initial stage in the evolution of parasitism, apparently due to relaxed selective constraint<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wicke, S. et al. Mechanistic model of evolutionary rate variation en route to a nonphotosynthetic lifestyle in plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 9045–9050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1367">13</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM7">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Loss of genes controlling flowering time</h3><p>Leaves play a critical role in perceiving environmental signals and thereby modulate the physiology of their own as well as other plant parts, such as activating flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Putterill, J. &amp; Varkonyi-Gasic, E. FT and florigen long-distance flowering control in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 33, 77–82 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1382">14</a></sup>. We speculate that the leafless dodders may have unique means of regulating flowering time and many flowering-time-control genes may have been lost. A database of flowering-time gene networks in <i>Arabidopsis</i> was recently constructed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Bouche, F., Lobet, G., Tocquin, P. &amp; Perilleux, C. FLOR-ID: an interactive database of flowering-time gene networks in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1167–D1171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1389">15</a></sup>. We found that among the 295 coding genes listed in this database, 26 are lost (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1c</a>), including the well-known <i>FLC</i>, <i>FRI</i>, <i>SVP</i>, <i>AGL17</i>, and <i>CO</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3b</a>). Moreover, the circadian clock genes <i>ELF3</i> and <i>4</i>, <i>ARR3</i> and <i>4</i>, and <i>CDF1</i> and <i>3</i> are also missing. <i>FKF1</i> and <i>CIB1</i>, which are essential in the photoperiod pathway, are also lost (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3b</a>). Flowering time is controlled by multiple pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Romera-Branchat, M., Andres, F. &amp; Coupland, G. Flowering responses to seasonal cues: what’s new? Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 21, 120–127 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1444">16</a></sup>, and it appears that the vernalization, temperature, autonomous, circadian clock, and photoperiod pathway all seem to be nonfunctional (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3b</a>). The regulation of <i>C. australis</i> flowering time is particularly interesting to explore further.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Loss of defense-related genes</h3><p>Leaves and roots are the most common sites of pathogen and insect infestation. We speculate that after the ancestor of <i>Cuscuta</i> became leaf- and rootless, reduced exposure to pathogens and insects relaxed selective constraints on defense-related genes, resulting in their eventual loss. Specifically, four gene families were inspected in detail (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3c</a>). <i>R</i> (resistance) genes are the critical components of plant immunity. Even though there is a possibility that the ancestor of Convolvulaceae experienced a reduction of <i>R</i> genes after the split between Convolvulaceae and other Solanales lineages, as <i>I. nil</i> has the smallest number of <i>R</i> genes (148; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3b</a>) among the 7Ref-Species, the reduction of <i>R</i> genes in <i>Cuscuta</i> is still drastic: <i>C. australis</i> genome harbors only 15 <i>R</i> genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#Fig3">3c</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3b</a>). Terpenes function as defenses against insects and pathogens. We predicted 9 terpene synthase genes (<i>TPS</i>s) in <i>C. australis</i>, and there are 31 to 60 <i>TPS</i>s in the 7Ref-Species (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3c</a>). Many P450 enzymes are involved in the biosynthesis of plant secondary metabolites, which are required for adaptation to biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Morant, M., Bak, S., Moller, B. L. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. Plant cytochromes P450: tools for pharmacology, plant protection and phytoremediation. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 14, 151–162 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1516">17</a></sup>. While more than 240 P450s are encoded by the genomes of the 7Ref-Species, <i>C. australis</i> genome harbors only 89 (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3d</a>). Receptor-like kinases (RLKs) are important in plant development and resistance to abiotic and biotic stresses. <i>C. australis</i> genome comprises only 339 <i>RLK</i> genes, much less than in the 7Ref-Species (at least 655 in potato) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3e</a>). Consistently, several well-studied genes in plant resistance to pathogens are also absent in <i>C. australis</i>, including <i>EDS1</i>, <i>EDS5</i>, <i>FMO1</i>, <i>SAG101</i>, and <i>PAD4</i>, which are essential for plant resistance to diseases, and <i>ALD1</i>, which is critical for systemic acquired resistance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Origin of haustorium</h3><p>The haustorium is a parasitic plant-specific organ, playing a critical role in establishing parasitism. <i>C. australis</i> and <i>C. pentagona</i> RNA-seq data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186–1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1572">6</a></sup> were assembled using the <i>C. australis</i> genome as the reference (mapping ratios 96% and 75%, respectively; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">9</a>). In <i>Cuscuta</i> prehaustoria and haustoria, we identified 2466 principally expressed genes (PEGs; at least one-fold greater than the average of the expression levels in all the other tissues) belonging to 1299 orthogroups (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1d</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3f</a>). GO analysis on these PEGs indicated enrichment of biological processes of metabolic process, transport, lignin and xyloglucan metabolism, and transcriptional regulation genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2d</a>). This is consistent with the haustorial function of transporting host substances and the findings that dynamic cell wall remodeling in parasite haustoria is important in the establishment of parasitism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analyses reveal core parasitism genes and suggest gene duplication and repurposing as sources of structural novelty. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 767–790 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1595">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Johnsen, H. R. et al. Cell wall composition profiling of parasitic giant dodder (Cuscuta reflexa) and its hosts: a priori differences and induced changes. New Phytol. 207, 805–816 (2015)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1595_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Olsen, S. et al. Getting ready for host invasion: elevated expression and action of xyloglucan endotransglucosylases/hydrolases in developing haustoria of the holoparasitic angiosperm Cuscuta. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 695–708 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1598">20</a></sup>. The biggest proportion of these 1299 corresponding orthologs’ principally expressed tissues in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>I. nil</i> were found to be the roots (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">9</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM4">1d</a>). These data imply that the evolution of <i>Cuscuta</i> haustorium may be related to expression changes of genes involved in root development. Similarly, comparative transcriptome analyses on three root parasites, <i>Triphysaria versicolor</i>, <i>Striga hermonthica</i>, and <i>Phelipanche aegyptiaca</i>, revealed that parasitism genes are derived primarily from root and floral tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analyses reveal core parasitism genes and suggest gene duplication and repurposing as sources of structural novelty. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 767–790 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1628">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Next, we performed a HYPHY analysis to obtain the genes that underwent positive selection and relaxed purifying selection after the divergence between the ancestors of <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Ipomoea</i> lineage, as these genes might be associated with the speciation of <i>Cuscuta</i> and evolution of parasitism. GO terms including “response to hormones”, “DNA methylation”, “regulation of transcription”, and “cell wall-related metabolism” were enriched from the 1124 positively selected genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3g</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2e</a>), and among these, 115 are principally expressed in prehaustoria/haustoria (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3f</a>), including a pectin esterase, receptor-like kinases, transcription factors, a serine carboxypeptidase, and transporters. We also found that 3890 genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3h</a>) exhibited signatures of relaxed purifying selection. The enriched GO terms include “terpenoid biosynthetic process”, “nitrate assimilation”, “photosystem II assembly”, and “regulation of signal transduction” (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2f</a>), and 504 genes with relaxed purifying selection (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3f</a>) are principally expressed in prehaustoria/haustoria, such as genes encoding subtilisin-like proteases and an ABC transporter.</p><p>Gene family expansion, mainly caused by gene duplication, often leads to neofunctionalization among the gene family members and is thought to be an important driving force in the acquisition of novel phenotypes. Thus, we performed GO analysis on the 3099 expanded gene family members of <i>C. australis</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3i</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM5">2g</a>). It was found that “response to auxin” and “DNA methylation” were enriched from the members of expanded gene families; among them, 109 genes are principally expressed in prehaustoria/haustoria (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3f</a>). These data are consistent with the finding that many haustorial genes in Orobanchaceae parasites also experienced relaxed purifying and/or positive selection and may play an important role in the evolution of the parasitic lifestyle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analyses reveal core parasitism genes and suggest gene duplication and repurposing as sources of structural novelty. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 767–790 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1678">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Five positively selected genes from the expanded gene families were found to be principally expressed in haustoria (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">10</a>. and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM6">3f</a>). Among these, one encodes a putative α/ß-hydrolase highly similar to <i>Nicotiana sylvestris</i> DAD2/DWARF14 (84% identity), which is the strigolactone receptor in autotrophic plants, implying that neofunctionalization of α/ß-hydrolase genes might also be involved in the parasitization process in <i>Cuscuta</i>, as they are in the root parasites <i>Striga</i> and <i>Orobanche</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Conn, C. E. et al. Convergent evolution of strigolactone perception enabled host detection in parasitic plants. Science 349, 540–543 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1703">21</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>How plants evolved parasitism is still unclear. Our transcriptomic data and molecular evolution analysis suggest convergent evolution in <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae root parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analyses reveal core parasitism genes and suggest gene duplication and repurposing as sources of structural novelty. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 767–790 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1719">18</a></sup>. The haustorium of <i>Cuscuta</i> probably evolved from root tissues and that of Orobanchaceae parasites recruited genes normally involved in the development of root and floral tissues. Moreover, a relatively large fraction of the genes that experienced positive selection and/or relaxed selection are principally expressed in prehausotria/haustoria in both <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae, and these genes may be related to parasitization and/or evolution of parasitism.</p><p>Our comparative genomic analyses indicate that the <i>C. australis</i> genome experienced remarkably high levels of contraction, and this is consistent with <i>Cuscuta</i>’s parasitic lifestyle and large changes in body plan—leaf- and rootless as well as intensive dependence on host-derived metabolites and signals. Given the importance of new genes that bring about novel phenotypes, it is possible that the autotrophic ancestor evolved the haustorium from root tissues through the neofunctionalization of duplicated genes and transcriptional reprogramming in the <i>Cuscuta</i> lineage. <i>Cuscuta</i> is transitioning from hemiparasitism to holoparasitism. Among the recognized major parasite lineages, three contain only hemiparasites, while eight are solely holoparasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Westwood, J. H., Yoder, J. I., Timko, M. P. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. The evolution of parasitism in plants. Trends Plant. Sci. 15, 227–235 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1744">1</a></sup>, implying that holoparasitism may have additional selective advantages over hemiparasitism. Hypothetically, the dramatic changes in body plans, including the degeneration of leaf and root could allow these parasites to reallocate carbon and nitrogen resources, which are required for their development and growth, to reproduction and could also enable the holoparasites to better adjust their physiology according to that of the hosts by eavesdropping on host signaling molecules.</p><p>Wicke et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wicke, S. et al. Mechanistic model of evolutionary rate variation en route to a nonphotosynthetic lifestyle in plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 9045–9050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1751">13</a></sup> analyzed the plastomes of nonparasitic, hemiparasitic, and nonphotosynthetic parasitic plants in Orobanchaceae, and found that the transition from autotrophic plants to obligate parasites relaxes functional constraints on plastid genes in a stepwise manner. Similar analyses could not be done in <i>Cuscuta</i>, as it is the only parasitic lineage in the Convolvulaceae. The large body plan changes that were associated with the parasitic lifestyle relaxed selective constraints on several core pathways, such as photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wicke, S. et al. Mechanistic model of evolutionary rate variation en route to a nonphotosynthetic lifestyle in plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 9045–9050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1758">13</a></sup>, flowering-time control, root and leaf development, nutrient acquirement, and defense against pathogens and insects. Under relaxed selection pressure, some genes of these pathways were pseudogenized and melted into the background of surrounding DNA because of accumulation of recurrent mutations, or were even deleted from the genome, finally leading to gene losses. In <i>C. australis</i> genome, 1168 genes were identified as pseudogenes, although some may still be functional or even have gained new functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Balakirev, E. S. &amp; Ayala, F. J. Pseudogenes: are they “junk” or functional DNA? Annu. Rev. Genet. 37, 123–151 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1765">22</a></sup>. It is likely that the majority of these pseudogenes have degenerated or are in the process of being deleted, following the fate of the lost genes. Indeed, <i>C. australis</i> genes that have undergone relaxed purifying selection include those related to terpenoid biosynthetic process (defense), nitrate assimilation (nutrient acquirement), and photosystem II assembly (photosynthesis). Resequencing of other <i>Cuscuta</i> species may shed light on the recent gene loss events and the underlying mechanisms (e.g., transposon insertion, fragment deletion, and rapid accumulation of mutations) in <i>Cuscuta</i>.</p><p>Large scale gene loss is also evident from the genomic data of human parasites <i>Pediculus humanus humanus</i> (body louse)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kirkness, E. F. et al. Genome sequences of the human body louse and its primary endosymbiont provide insights into the permanent parasitic lifestyle. Proc. Natl.Acad. Sci. USA 107, 12168–12173 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1785">23</a></sup>, <i>Trichinella spiralis</i> (a zoonotic nematode)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Mitreva, M. et al. The draft genome of the parasitic nematode Trichinella spiralis. Nat. Genet. 43, 228–235 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1792">24</a></sup>, and <i>Giardia lamblia</i> (an intestinal protist)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Morrison, H. G. et al. Genomic minimalism in the early diverging intestinal parasite Giardia lamblia. Science 317, 1921–1926 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1800">25</a></sup>, and from an <i>Arabidopsis</i> pathogen, the obligate biotrophic oomycete <i>Hyaloperonospora arabidopsidis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Baxter, L. et al. Signatures of adaptation to obligate biotrophy in the Hyaloperonospora arabidopsidis genome. Science 330, 1549–1551 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1809">26</a></sup>. Here, we also detected that <i>U. gibba</i> also experienced a large number of gene loss events during its evolution, likely resulting from its body plan changes (mainly loss of root) and lifestyle alteration (carnivory). It would also be of importance to compare the genomes of <i>Cuscuta</i> and those of Orobanchaceae root parasites. We expect that Orobanchaceae parasites, especially holoparasites, such as <i>Orobanche</i> species, may also have a large number of lost nuclear genes, some of which may bear the same functions as those in <i>Cuscuta</i>, such as the genes that function in leaf development and photosynthesis. The <i>C. australis</i> genomic data strongly support the notion that regressive evolution, which is associated with extensive gene loss, is likely to be pervasive and adaptive during the evolution of holo- /obligate parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Albalat, R. &amp; Canestro, C. Evolution by gene loss. Nat. Rev. Genet. 17, 379–391 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1829">27</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wolf, Y. I. &amp; Koonin, E. V. Genome reduction as the dominant mode of evolution. Bioessays 35, 829–837 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1832">28</a></sup>. Comparative genomics between the genome data of <i>C. australis</i> and autotrophic plants provides an important resource for studying genome reduction, regressive evolution, parasitism, and evo-devo in plant parasites.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">DNA sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>The seeds of <i>C. australis</i> were originally purchased from a Chinese traditional medicinal herbs store in Kunming, China, in 2011, and had been cultivated for five generations in a glasshouse at the Kunming Institute of Botany, Chinese Academy of Sciences. Voucher specimens of <i>C. australis</i> can be accessed at the Herbarium of the Kunming Institute of Botany, Chinese Academy of Sciences (accessions Nos. WJQ-001-1 and WJQ-001-3). Seedlings were prepared from seeds of the fifth generation and were infested on soybean plants (for the germination procedure, see Li et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, J. et al. The parasitic plant Cuscuta australis Is highly insensitive to abscisic acid-induced suppression of hypocotyl elongation and seed germination. PLoS ONE&#xA;                           10, e0135197 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1857">29</a></sup>). DNA isolated from young vines collected from one individual <i>C. australis</i> was used for genomic library construction. Three genomic DNA libraries with 350-bp, 2-kb, or 5-kb insertions were constructed for Illumina sequencing. For PacBio sequencing, five DNA libraries with 20-kb insertions were sequenced on a PacBio RS II instrument using the P6v2 polymerase binding and C4 chemistry kits (P6-C4). A total of 26.6 Gb from 1,953,966 reads were obtained by processing 24 single-molecule real-time (SMRT) cells. The average and N50 of SMRT subread length were 9.6 kb and 13.6 kb, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Transcriptome sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p><i>C. australis</i> tissues of seeds, just-germinated seeds (1 day after imbibing), seedlings (5 days after imbibing), prehaustoria, haustoria, stems far from haustoria, stems near haustoria, flower buds, flowers, and seed capsules were collected and RNA samples were extracted from these tissues using the TRI Reagent (Sigma). Libraries were constructed for each tissue according to the TrueSeq<sup>®</sup> RNA Sample Preparation protocol, and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq-2500. Sequences are deposited in NCBI under BioProject PRJNA394036.</p><p><i>C. pentagona</i> transcriptomic short reads dataset from Ranjan et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186–1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1881">6</a></sup> were retrieved from the NCBI Short Read Archive under accession numbers SRR965929, SRR965963, SRR966236, SRR966405, SRR966412, SRR966513, SRR966542, SRR966549, SRR966619 to SRR966622, SRR967154, SRR967164, SRR967181 to SRR967190, SRR967275 to SRR967289, and SRR967291.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome survey</h3><p>We used 23.29 Gb of HiSeq reads to estimate the genome features using the GCE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Preprint at https://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1893">30</a></sup> (v1.0.0) software based on <i>k</i>-mer depth-frequency distribution. A total of 11,700,637,064 17-mers were counted. Given the unique <i>k</i>-mer depth of 42, we calculated that the genome size = KmerCount/Depth = 272.57 Mb. The repeat content was estimated to be 58.94% based on <i>k</i>-mer depth distribution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">De novo assembly</h3><p>SMRT subreads were corrected, trimmed, and assembled using CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1914">31</a></sup> (v1.3), a genome assembler built on the basis of Celera assembler. Briefly, SMRT subreads were firstly self-corrected based on an overlap-layout algorithm. Erroneous regions of error-corrected SMRT subreads were then trimmed to increase accuracy. We constructed an initial assembly using CANU with those trimmed error-corrected SMRT subreads following the parameters “genomeSize = 273 m errorRate = 0.025” and then used Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563-569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1918">32</a></sup> (v4.0) to generate consensus sequences by aligning SMRT subreads to correct the errors in the assembly. Lastly, we used Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1922">33</a></sup> (v1.18) to perform the second round of error correction with HiSeq reads from the 350-bp-insert library. The resulted error-corrected assembly was named version 1.0 and used in the subsequent analyses. A hierarchical method was also applied to concatenate adjacent contigs: SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1926">34</a></sup> (v 3.0) was first used to build scaffolds using HiSeq data from the mate-pair libraries and the contigs built from the PacoBio data. N50 of the resulted scaffolds reached 5.9 Mb. SSPACE-LongRead<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1930">35</a></sup> (v1-1) was further applied to build superscaffolds with PacBio long reads, nevertheless, no linking information between scaffolds were found and no improvements were acquired (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Assembly assessment</h3><p>The accuracy and heterozygosity rate of the genome assembly were estimated using the following procedure: adaptors were removed from the short paired-end reads obtained from the 350-bp insert size library, and then the reads were aligned to the PacBio assembly, which had been corrected with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1947">33</a></sup> (v1.18), using bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1951">36</a></sup> (v 2.2.4) to generate a bam file. A total of 97.9% reads could be mapped. Samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1955">37</a></sup> (v 1.3.1) were used to sort the bam file, and Freebayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46651e1959">38</a></sup> (v1.1.0) was applied to call variants. Compared with PacBio assembly as the reference genome, homozygous SNPs or indels were considered to be assembly errors, and heterozygous SNPs or indels were regarded as heterozygous sites. The accuracy was estimated to be 99.99% and heterozygosity was estimated to be 0.013% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8#MOESM1">3</a>). Error rate of the genome assembly before Pilon correction was also estimated using the same set of methods. A total of 980 SNPs and 101825 Indels were corrected after polishing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Data availability</h3><p>The genome assembly, gene models, and sequence reads are available at the NCBI under the BioProject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA394036">PRJNA394036</a>, and the former two can also be accessed at <a href="http://www.dodderbase.org">http://www.dodderbase.org</a>. Phylogenetic analysis data for detection of gene losses, gene alignments used for selection analysis, and pseudogene annotations can be accessed at <a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6072131">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.6072131</a>. All data are also available from the corresponding author upon request.</p></div></div></section>

                    <i>Nat Commun</i> <b>9, </b>2683 (2018). https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-04721-8</p><p class="c-bibliographic-information__download-citation u-hide-print"><a data-test="citation-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="download article citation" data-track-label="link" data-track-external="" href="/articles/s41467-018-04721-8.ris">Download citation<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-download"></use></svg></a></p><ul class="c-bibliographic-information__list" data-test="publication-history"><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Received<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2017-08-08">08 August 2017</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Accepted<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2018-04-23">23 April 2018</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Published<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2018-07-11">11 July 2018</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item c-bibliographic-information__list-item--doi"><p><abbr title="Digital Object Identifier">DOI</abbr><span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-04721-8" data-track="click" data-track-action="view doi" data-track-label="link" itemprop="sameAs">https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-04721-8</a></span></p></li></ul><div data-component="share-box"></div><div data-component="article-info-list"></div></div></div></div></div></section>
